(without replacement)  Answer by venugopalramana(3286)   ( Show Source ): You can put this solution on YOUR website! THERE ARE 26 RED CARDS OUT OF 52. HENCE PROBABILITY OF DRAWING RED CARD FIRST=26/52=1/2 NOW THERE ARE 26 BLACK CARDS AND 51 TOTAL CARDS PROBABILITY OF DRAWING BLACK CARD IS =26/51 SO PROBABILITY OF DRAWING FIRST RED AND THEN BLACK CARD =(1/2)(26/51) THIS ENDS YOUR PROBLEM. ***************************************************************************** BUT IF PROBILITY OF ONE RED AND ONE BLACK CARD IS NEEDED THEN..... SIMILARLY PROBABILITY OF DRAWING FIRST BLACK AND THEN RED CARD =(1/2)(26/51) HENCE TOTAL PROBABILITY OF DRAWING ONE BLACK AND ONE RED CARD =SUM OF THE ABOVE 2 PROBABILITIES.=26/51                                                                                          0      0     1              My Profile  Logout       Settings   E-mail & Password    Avatar    Signature    Notification Settings    Global Settings     Applicant profile   Update status    My GMAT info    My Schools        My workspace   My Bookmarks    My Notes    Subscription Feed       My Follow Feed  Kudos  My Workbook      Messages and Replies   Settings  Mark All Read    See All     Applicant Notifications   Settings  Mark All Read    See All     Global notifications   Settings  Mark All Read    See All                       Forgot password? But the way to solve this would be to :  - count the total number of red cards. - Remember that there will be some #7 cards that are red. The only thing I know is there're 52 in a deck and 4 shapes.  But the way to solve this would be to : - count the total number of red cards. - Remember that there will be some #7 cards that are red. So you need to discount these. - Add these two items to give favoable outcomes - Divide this by 52 to give you the probability   there are equal red and black cards = 26 each color. there are four 7s = 4, of which 2 are already in red. so total number of cards either red or 7s=26+4-2=28 prob=28/52=7/13                           krisrini      Senior Manager           Joined: 15 Apr 2005   Posts: 414   Location: India, Chennai                       Re: Easy Probability [ #permalink ]     Show Tags     06 Oct 2005, 23:05       chets wrote: What is the probability of picking a red card or a 7, if 1 card is selected randomly from a deck of 52 cards. a) 1/2 b) 1/13 c) 6/13 d) 7/13 e) 25/52   P(A or B) = P(A) + P(B) - P(A and B) Where A is picking a red card, and B is picking the # 7  Since there are 26 Red cards in a deck of 52 cards. P(A) = 26/52  There are 4 - 7's in the deck of 52 cards.  Hence P(B) = 4/52  Of the 4 - 7's 2 of them are in red and 2 in black.  So p(choosing a red card and the number being 7 ) = 2/52  Sustituting we get = (26/52) + (4/52) - (2/52) = 28/52 = 7/13                         macca      Senior Manager           Joined: 22 Jun 2005   Posts: 355   Location: London                        [ #permalink ]     Show Tags     07 Oct 2005, 02:13      I'd say D. Quora Ask New Question Sign In  Playing Cards  Probability (statistics)  Statistics (academic discipline) What is the probability of drawing a red card from a standard pack of playing cards? Update Cancel ad by UC Berkeley Extension Want to learn basic statistic concepts? Sign up for introduction to statistics at UC Berkeley Extension. Classroom & online courses available. Learn More at extension.berkeley.edu You dismissed this ad. The feedback you provide will help us show you more relevant content in the future. Undo Answer Wiki 3 Answers   Bill Hazelton , PhD from University of Melbourne (1992) Answered Aug 30, 2017 · Author has 977 answers and 639.1k answer views Nancy Mitchell’s answer is quite correct. An alternative answer would be that if the pack contained one or two jokers, as well as the 52 regular cards, you would have a probability of 26/53 or 26/54 = 13/27 for one and two jokers, respectively. It depends upon your definition of a ‘standard pack’ of playing cards. Given that there may be additional cards, such as blanks and advertising, these values may change. Buy a few different packs of new cards and see what’s inside. 537 Views Related Questions More Answers Below What is the probability of drawing all red cards from a standard deck of 52 cards if you draw 5 cards from the deck? You draw 3 cards from a standard deck of 52 cards. what is the probability of getting 3 red cards? A card is randomly drawn from a standard deck of playing cards. Undo Answer Wiki 3 Answers Quora User , Earned 98% or higher in all my math classes at UCMO. Answered Jun 12, 2017 · Author has 1.8k answers and 358.8k answer views This is the number of ways to get 5 red cards out of 26, divided by the number of ways to get 5 cards out of 52. I’ll do it the long way instead of using a calculator because I feel like it today. [math]\dfrac{\dbinom{26}{5}}{\dbinom{52}{5}}[/math] [math]\cfrac{\left(\cfrac{26!}{5!21!}\right)}{\left(\cfrac{52!}{5!47!}\right)}[/math] [math]\dfrac{26!47!}{21!52!}[/math] [math]\dfrac{(26\cdot 25\cdot 24\cdot 23\cdot 22\cdot 21!)\cdot 47!}{21!\cdot (52\cdot 51\cdot 50\cdot 49\cdot 48\cdot 47!)}[/math] [math]\dfrac{26\cdot 25\cdot 24\cdot 23\cdot 22}{52\cdot 51\cdot 50\cdot 49\cdot 48}[/math] Cancelling the 26 with the 52, the 25 with the 50, and the 24 with the 48: [math]\dfrac{23\cdot 22}{2\cdot 51\cdot 2\cdot 49\cdot 2}[/math] Cancelling one of the 2s in the denominator with the 22 in the numerator: [math]\dfrac{23\cdot 11}{51\cdot 2\cdot 49\cdot 2}[/math] Performing the multiplication: [math]\dfrac{253}{9996}[/math] I find it somewhat interesting that the answer is very close to 250/10000 which is 1/40, but it is just slightly higher. 399 Views · View Upvoters Related Questions More Answers Below What is the probability of drawing a red card from a standard pack of playing cards? Undo   Bartholomew Wolinski , studies Computer Engineering & Mathematics at Stony Brook University (2019) Answered Jun 12, 2017 A standard deck of 52 cards has 26 red cards (13 hearts, and 13 diamonds, as well as 26 black cards (13 spades, as well as 13 clubs). The probability of drawing all red cards out of a standard deck would be ((26 choose 5)* (25 choose 4) * (24 choose 3)* (23 choose 2)* (22 choose 1))/(52 choose 5). You divide the numerator by the denominator because you are finding probability and with these types of questions, you divide what you're looking for by all outcomes. With the numerator, it's any 5 of the 26 red cards and order is not important so you are just choosing 26 out of 5, then 25 out of 4, then 24 out of 3, then 23 out of 2, and finally one red card out of the 22 available cards. 168 Views Related Questions What is the probability of drawing a queen and a 7 from a deck of 52 cards? Undo Answer Wiki 3 Answers   Kurusar Iyer , works at Freelancing Answered Jun 20, 2017 · Author has 109 answers and 151.6k answer views There are 4 suits, Hearts ♥, Spades ♠, Diamonds ♦, Clubs ♣ in a standard deck of 52 cards. Out of 4, Hearts and Diamonds suits are red in color. Each suits consist 13 cards from Ace to King. It means there's only one King of Hearts (K ♥ ) and King of Diamonds (K ♦). Total there are 2 king of red color out of 52 cards. Therefore, the probability of getting a king of red color will be 2/52 or 1/26. 6.3k Views · View Upvoters Related Questions More Answers Below If a card is drawn at random from a deck of 52 cards, what is the probability that the card is red or a king? Undo Answer Wiki 9 Answers Quora User , IT guy, single dad, sports fan, observer of pop culture, know a little about a lot of different things. Answered Mar 10, 2016 · Author has 4.3k answers and 6.2m answer views Originally Answered: What is the probability of drawing a red or even in a deck of cards? (if Ace, Jack, and King are considered odd and Queen is considered even Assuming standard deck of 52 cards, no jokers. There are 26 red cards in the deck. All the diamonds and all the hearts. There are 24 even cards in the deck -- 2, 4, 6, 8, 10, Q of each suit. However, you don't want to double-count cards that are both red AND even, so you take back 12 cards (2, 4, 6, 8, 10, Q of the red suits) from the combined total and only count the even black cards. So 12 new cards that fit the criteria. So you have 26 + 12 = 38 unique cards that satisfy the conditions, out of 52 total cards. For a total of .730769... decide how many significant figures you care about, but... a little less than a 3/4ths chance. ( Leaving the jokers in just drops it to 38/54 ) 8.6k Views Thank you for your feedback! Your response is private. Is this answer still relevant and up to date? Ask New Question   Tony Mechelynck , retired math teacher (junior high school) Answered Oct 4, 2015 · Author has 72 answers and 100.1k answer views Well, let's assume that all cards have the same probability of being drawn. You didn't say which deck of cards, though. 32 or 52 cards? With no joker, 1 joker, or 2 jokers? I'll assume 52 cards with no jokers; after you read the answer below you should be able to compute it for other kinds of decks, even a French deck of tarots, where each colour (hearts, diamonds, clubs and spades) includes a 14th card called the "knight" and ranked between the jack and queen, and there are 22 cards which are neither red nor black (the trumps, which we could regard approximately as 22 jokers numbered 0 to 21). So, which cards are "red or even" (ah, yes, and I take the conjunction "or" in its inclusive sense, i.e., red or even or both)? All hearts, 13 cards All diamonds, 13 cards 2, 4, 6, 8, 10, Q of clubs, 6 cards 2, 4, 6, 8, 10, Q of spades, 6 cards Total 38 mutually symmetrical disjoint "favourable" cases out of 52 "possible" cases. The answer is 38/52 or 19/26 i.e. 0.730769 ± 5.10^-7 All right. Now to see if you have understood, you compute the probability of drawing a red or even card from a deck of tarots as defined above. 1.6k Views Thank you for your feedback! Your response is private. Undo   Alexander Farrugia , Ph.D. Mathematics, University of Malta (2016) Answered Aug 8, 2015 · Author has 2.1k answers and 7.2m answer views The probability of drawing a red card is [math]\frac{1}{2}[/math] . The probability of drawing an even card is [math]\frac{6}{13}[/math] . The probability of drawing a red and even card is [math]\frac{1}{2}\times\frac{6}{13}=\frac{3}{13}[/math] . If you mean "red or even" in the sense of "or both", then the probability of drawing a red or even card is [math]\frac{1}{2}+\frac{6}{13}-\frac{3}{13}[/math] , i.e. we're using [math]P(A\cup B)=P(A)+P(B)-P(A\cap B)[/math] So [math]P(red or even or both) = \frac{1}{2}+\frac{3}{13}=\frac{19}{26}[/math] . If you mean "red or even" in the sense of "but not both", then the probability of drawing a red or even card is [math]\frac{19}{26}-\frac{3}{13}[/math] , i.e. we're finding [math]P(A\cup B) - P(A\cap B).[/math] So [math]P(red or even, but not both) = \frac{19}{26}-\frac{3}{13}=\frac{1}{2}.[/math] 3.5k Views · View Upvoters Thank you for your feedback! Your response is private. Is this answer still relevant and up to date?   Mark Neuhausen , Serial Entrepreneur, Tech Partner, Angel Investor Answered Oct 4, 2015 · Author has 2.2k answers and 1.6m answer views You have two different answers, and both are correct depending on whether you want the OR in your question to be inclusive or exclusive.  In logic, it is typically inclusive (red or even would include even red cards).  In the English language, or is typically exclusive not matching logic (cake or ice cream, with an answer of yes considered to mean both). 2k Views   Terry Moore , PhD in statistics Answered Aug 9, 2016 · Author has 3.9k answers and 1.5m answer views In each suit, out of Ace and the cards 2 to 10, there are 5 odd and 5 even numbers. Jack and King add 2 more odd numbers and Queen adds an even number. So there are 7 odd numbers and 6 even numbers in each suit. So the chance of an even number is 24/52 (taking account of the four suits). Out of 52 cards 24 are even and 26 are red. But 12 are both. So the probability is 24/52 + 26/52 - 12/52 = 38/52 = 19/26. (If you didn’t subtract 12/52 you would have counted the even red cards twice.) 760 Views · Answer requested by  Heather Escoe promoted  by Wharton Executive Education Learn business analytics online with Wharton Executive Education. Undo   Robert Kaspar , PhD, (electro)chemical engineering Answered Aug 8, 2015 · Author has 408 answers and 729.6k answer views It's simply the probability of drawing a red plus the probability of drawing an even, except that you double-count some cards that way, so you have to subtract off the probability of drawing a card that's both red *and* even. You can calculate these three probabilities by counting the number of cards in each category and dividing by the total number of cards.  In a deck of 52, 26 are red, 24 are even, and 12 are both red and even. So the answer is 26/52 + 24/52 - 12/52 = 38/52 ≈ 0.731 2.4k Views · View Upvoters   Partha Chattopadhyay (পার্থ চট্টোপাধ্যায়) , former  Civil Servant & former lecturer in a college Answered Feb 25, 2018 · Author has 654 answers and 355.2k answer views There are 26 red cards (13 hearts & 13 diamonds), 24 even cards (6 each of clubs, diamonds, hearts and spades). Now, probability of drawing a red card = 26/52=1/2. Probability of drawing an even card = 24/52 6/13. And probability of drawing a red even card = 12/52 = 3/13 . Hence, the required probability is 1/2 + 6/13 - 3/13 = 19/26. 94 Views · Answer requested by  Kusum Juyal   Anthony Clohesy , Maths Graduate, High School Maths teacher Answered Aug 8, 2015 · Author has 109 answers and 517.4k answer views The important thing to notice here is that 'red' and 'even' are not mutually exclusive outcomes. That is, they could occur simultaneously (Eg 6 of hearts). So if you are tempted to add the probabilities of each together, you should then subtract the chance of both since it will have been double counted. Red: 26/52 Even: 24/52 Both: 12/52 Either: 26/52 + 24/52 - 12/52 = 38/52 = 19/26. Alternatively list all the cards that satisfy the requirements: 26 red cards and 12 black even cards gives 38 cards in total. 38/52 as before. 1.1k Views   Mike Richmond , Studied physics Answered Aug 8, 2015 · Author has 5.6k answers and 8.9m answer views Originally Answered: If the Ace, Jack, and the King is considered odd and the Queen is considered even, what is the probability of drawing a red or even, in a deck of cards? Answers  All  Blogs  Files  Lessons  Videos        Ask a question                   0                          Ask a Question   From a standard deck of cards, what is the probability that you draw a red card, replace it and then draw a heart?      Answer choices  A) 1/8  B) 3/8  C) 3/4  D) 2       Probability       10/12/2014 |  Idk from Philadelphia, PA      Subscribe          Comment              1 Answer by Expert Tutors  Tutors, sign in to answer this question.          Lynne R. | Here to help with Math! Here to help with Math!     5.0   5.0  (450 lesson ratings)  (450)               1            The answer is A.  The probability that you draw a red card is 26/52 or 1/2, since half the cards in the deck are red.    Since you replace the card, the probabiity that you draw a heart is 13/52 or 1/4, since a quarter of the cards are hearts.    Since you want to know the probability of BOTH events happening, you multiply the two.    One half times one fourth is 1/8, or A.     10/12/2014 | Lynne R. Please Explain     Math Word Problem       4/9/2015 |  Marite from Burlington, NC      Subscribe          Comment              2 Answers by Expert Tutors  Tutors, sign in to answer this question.          LARRY W. | I have been a MATH tutor for over 45 years-Alg I & II, Geom. I have been a MATH tutor for over 45 yea...                1             Marked as Best Answer    Marite:    Maybe this will help you.  You have a 6 out of 52 cards that will satisfy the first draw of a red face card (J, Q, K of hearts and diamonds).  In the second draw, you have 26 out of 52 cards (or, 1/2) that will be black.  So multiply the two answers together, and you have 6/52 times 1/2 = probability of 3 out of 52, or 3/52.  Better??    Larry     4/10/2015 | LARRY W. Answers  All  Blogs  Files  Lessons  Videos        Ask a question                   0                          Ask a Question   Find the probability of drawing a red card numbers 2 through 10 on the first draw, replacing it and drawing a face card on the second draw.      Please Help       Math Word Problem       4/9/2015 |  Marite from Burlington, NC      Subscribe          Comment              1 Answer by Expert Tutors  Tutors, sign in to answer this question.          Mark H. | Tutoring in Math and Science at all levels Tutoring in Math and Science at all leve...     4.8   4.8  (42 lesson ratings)  (42)               0             Marked as Best Answer    OK!!    Assume there are no jokers    52 cards in four suits---26 red and 26 black  numbers are 2 - 10, then J, Q, K, A  (13 choices)    There are 3 face cards in each suit, for a total of 12 face cards    Numbers 2 - 10 inclusive = 9 choices for each suit.   (18 choices for a red 2-10)    first draw:   18 choices out of 52---probability = 18/52    assume the card is returned and the deck is shuffled---this means that there is no correlation between the 1st and 2nd draws--they are independent events.    second draw:  12 choices out of 52----probability = 12/52    Combined probability = (18/52)(12/52) = 0.0799     4/9/2015 | Mark H. Answers  All  Blogs  Files  Lessons  Videos        Ask a question                   0                          Ask a Question   A card is drawn from a well-shuffled deck of 52 cards. Find the probability that the card is a red card or a 3.      A card is drawn from a well-shuffled deck of 52 cards. Find the probability that the card is a red card or a 3.     Math Word Problem Word Problems Math Help Mathematics Finite Mathematics Finite Finite Math Word Problem Help       3/28/2015 |  Kelsey from Silver Spring, MD      Subscribe          Comment              1 Answer by Expert Tutors  Tutors, sign in to answer this question.          Arthur D. | Effective Mathematics Tutor Effective Mathematics Tutor     5.0   5.0  (7 lesson ratings)  (7)      Message             0            there are 13 hearts and 13 diamonds for a total of 26 red cards  there are four 3's but two of them are red so you can't count them twice !!!  P(red)= 26/52  P(3) =4/52-2/52=2/52 (remember that two 3's are red and we already counted them in the 26/52)  add the two probabilities because of the "or"  P =26/52+2/52=28/52=7/13 in simplest form     3/28/2015 |  Arthur D.
